    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f963ad6057254df99d5434a55aa88d86">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1f4d7dd6-4008-4b56-9e88-84796a5607aa'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zhaoyun@sxu.edu.cn"><span>zhaoyun@sxu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:tianwenjuan@sxu.edu.cn"><span>tianwenjuan@sxu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zongping.shao@curtin.edu.au"><span>zongping.shao@curtin.edu.au</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Tian/Wenjuan">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Qi/Xingguo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xingguo Qi</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-name">Xingguo Qi</p>
                     <p>Shanxi Huana Carbon Energy Technology Co. Ltd., Taiyuan, 030006 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Qi/Xingguo">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Han/Gaoyi" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Gaoyi Han</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">

High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/advs.202303108">https://doi.org/10.1002/advs.202303108</a></div><div class="epub-section cited-by-count"><span>Citations: <a    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d149124980" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Crystal transformation of metal compound cathodes during charge/discharge processes in alkali metal-ion batteries usually generates profound impact on structural stability and electrochemical performance, while the theme in anode materials, which always occurs and completes during the first redox cycle, is rarely explored probably due to the fast transformation dynamics. Herein, for the first time, a unique crystal transformation behavior with slow dynamics in anode of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is reported, which further promotes electrochemical performance. Specifically, irreversible γ → β crystal transformation of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> is observed, induced by the persistent size degradation of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> particles during repeated sodiation/desodiation, supported by a series of ex situ characterizations, such as HRTEM, XRD, and XPS of γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/reduced graphene oxide (γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO) nanocomposite. The hybrid electrode shows ultrahigh long-term cycling stability (378&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles) and excellent rate capability (272&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Full battery with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode also manifests superior performance, promising β-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> dominated electrode materials in high-power and long-life SIBs. The first-principle calculations suggest the crystal transformation enhances electric conductivity of β-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and facilitates its accessibility to sodium. In combination with the synergistic effect between rGO matrix, substantially enhanced electrochemical performance is realized.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6229-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6229-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The next-generation electric power system based on renewable energies is one of the fundamental solutions to alleviate global fossil energy exhaustion and the issues of excessive carbon emissions. Because of the intermittent nature and scattering region dispersion of the renewable power sources, such as solar, wind, and tide energies, large-scale energy storage devices are indispensable to guarantee the grid-connection stability and the flexibility to terminal consumers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0001_R_d149124960e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0002_R_d149124960e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Renew. Sust. Energ. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Amidst of the promising large-scale energy storage technologies, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) stand out by the virtues of high energy density, high operation voltage, long cycling life, and low cost. Moreover, the similar working principle to that in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and the abundant sodium resources make SIBs be highly competitive for commercial applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0003_R_d149124960e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a>-<a href="#advs6229-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0007_R_d149124960e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the practical applications of SIBs are still frustrated by the deficiency of commercially suitable electrode materials. Especially, the commonly used artificial/natural graphites as commercial anode materials of LIBs are failed in SIBs due to the thermodynamic instability of the sodium-graphite intercalation compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0008_R_d149124960e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0009_R_d149124960e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>For promoting the commercial applications of SIBs, tremendous efforts have been made to exploit high-performance anode materials, such as hard carbon, soft carbon, and metal chalcogenides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0010_R_d149124960e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small">10</a>-<a href="#advs6229-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0012_R_d149124960e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Carbon">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, metal selenides (MSs) with conversion or/and alloy type sodium-storage mechanisms are receiving ever-increasing research interests owing to their high theoretical specific capacity, safe working voltage platform, and environmental friendly nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0013_R_d149124960e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the weaker covalent bonds and narrower band gaps of MSs than that for the corresponding metal oxides and sulfides result in easier sodiation/desodiation reaction kinetics and better electric conductivity. Therefore, many successful cases, such as CoSe<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0014_R_d149124960e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MoSe<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0015_R_d149124960e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SnSe,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0016_R_d149124960e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0017_R_d149124960e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Small">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been demonstrated to manifest favorable sodium-storage performance. Nevertheless, like other metal-based compounds with high capacity (e.g., sulfides and phosphides), the intrinsic low electron/ion conductivity and enormous volume fluctuation upon conversion/alloy reactions always remain to be challenging for desirable performance. For promoting electrochemical performance, the rational nanostructure design and the combination with high-conductive carbon materials (carbon coatings or carbon matrices) have been widely practiced as the most effective strategies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0018_R_d149124960e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Nano">18</a>-<a href="#advs6229-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0022_R_d149124960e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, other potential strategies that may boost the electrode capacity and cycling stability are also intensively searched after.</p>
         
         <p>It is well known that crystal phase transformation behaviors of metal compound electrode materials during charge/discharge processes exert a profound influence on structural stability and electrochemical performance for alkali-metal ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0023_R_d149124960e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Mater.Today">23</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0024_R_d149124960e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These phenomena always occur in cathode materials with the induction of extraction of alkali-metal ions at high charge voltage. For example, during the delithiation process of LiCoO<sub>2</sub> cathodes, the migration of cobalt ions into lithium layers leads to the phase transition of layered structure to spinel and rock-salt structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0025_R_d149124960e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Tremendous interests have been drawn to the researches of phase evolutions in sodium-based transition metal layered oxide cathodes (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) in recent years. The dominating P2-type and O3-type phase structures for Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> are transformed to O2 and P3 separately at the deeply charged states (above 4.2&nbsp;V) due to the gliding of oxygen layers, which unfortunately always result in the lattice mismatch and dislocations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0026_R_d149124960e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">26</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0027_R_d149124960e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a consequence, the irreversible phase transitions give rise to a series of detrimental effects, including the deteriorated electrode structures and degraded cycling stability. Therefore, normally such crystal transformations should be avoided or suppressed through limiting the operation voltage window and cation doping in transition metal sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0027_R_d149124960e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Surprisingly, the systematic crystal phase transformation investigations were rarely carried out in the literatures relating to metal compound anode materials, especially for SIBs, which could be resulted from the too fast phase transformation dynamics during electrochemical redox processes according to the reported cases currently. For example, a rapid crystal transformation of MnS from α to β phase was observed during the first discharge/charge cycle in LIBs, while the analogous behavior did not appear in the case of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0028_R_d149124960e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And recently, a fast phase transformation from Cu<sub>2</sub>Se to Cu<sub>2-x</sub>Se was also discovered in the sodiation/desodiation process of the first cycle, but the related intensive studies were scarce.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0029_R_d149124960e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0030_R_d149124960e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We speculate these metal compounds with rich crystal phases may have a higher probability for the occurrence of crystal transformation upon redox reactions. Similar to MnS, In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> is a typical polymorphic chalcogenide that has various of phase structures such as α, β, γ, δ, and к.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0031_R_d149124960e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an important member in MSs, In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> is a typical III-VI chalcogenide semiconductor with a 2D layered structure in most of cases. Benefitting from the featured bandgap of 1.3–2.0&nbsp;eV, it has become hotspots in the fields of solar cells,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0032_R_d149124960e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> photocatalysis,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0033_R_d149124960e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Appl. Catal. B">33</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0034_R_d149124960e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Catal.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> phase change memory,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0035_R_d149124960e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Solid State Electron">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and gas sensors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0036_R_d149124960e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the electrochemical energy storage applications, In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> also exhibits great potentials due to its competitive reversible capacity and high volumetric energy density. However, up to now, there are still few reports about the studies relating to In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> based electrode materials for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0037_R_d149124960e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Nano">37</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0038_R_d149124960e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Acta Phys. –Chim. Sin.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this study, for the first time, we reported a unique and beneficial crystal transformation phenomenon in SIBs by employing In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> as the anode material. Considering the low intrinsic conductivity, it was coupled with reduced graphene oxide (rGO) through a facile wet chemistry method. In particular, one kind of γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposite was developed as a superb prototype. The repeated sodiation/desodiation induced crystal transformation was systematically investigated by various material characterizations, especially the ex-situ means such as HRTEM observation and XRD/XPS analyses. The electrochemical behavior and kinetics investigation demonstrated that the γ → β crystal transition of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> contributed to the ultra-stable cycling ability and excellent rate performance. Such beneficial crystal transformation then provides another strategy for the development of competitive anodes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6229-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6229-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6229-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6229-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO Nanocomposites</h3>
            
            <p>The γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites were prepared by first liquid-phase mixing of In(OH)<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles (NPs) and GO and then conducting a gas phase selenization treatment, as schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6229-fig-0001">1</a>. In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs were synthesized via a solvothermal route with DMF/H<sub>2</sub>O mixture as the solvent, which provided a weak basic medium based on the hydrolysis mechanism:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0039_R_d149124960e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Colloid Interf. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
               <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e92ef583-7639-408f-8349-93b628b5296d/advs6229-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6229:advs6229-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6229-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>HOCN</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>CH</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                           </mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <mo>+</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                        <mo>→</mo>
                        <mi>HOCNH</mi>
                        <msubsup>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>CH</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                           </mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msubsup>
                        <mo>+</mo>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>OH</mi>
                           <mo>−</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{HOCN}}{({\mathrm{CH}}_3)}_2 + {{\mathrm{H}}}_2{\mathrm{O}} \to {\mathrm{HOCNH}}({\mathrm{CH}}_3)_2^ + + {\mathrm{OH}}^ - $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, to conduct the nucleation and crystal growth of the In(OH)<sub>3</sub> species. Meanwhile, PVP was added in to play the roles of surface stabilizer and particle dispersant,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0040_R_d149124960e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Dalton Trans.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> contributing to the small dimension of the resulting In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs. In(OH)<sub>3</sub>@GO nanocomposite was then obtained by mixing In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs and GO in distilled water, then quenching in liquid N<sub>2</sub> and finally conducting freeze-drying. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs were evenly dispersed on the surface of GO, and the dimension of most of the particles was less than 50&nbsp;nm. The high purity of these In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs was verified by XRD analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, the In(OH)<sub>3</sub>@GO nanocomposite was mixed with selenium powder and heat-treated in an Ar atmosphere to realize the transformation from In(OH)<sub>3</sub> phase to γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> phase. The possible selenization mechanism is as: 1) 2In(OH)<sub>3</sub> → In<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 3H<sub>2</sub>O; 2) 2In<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>  + 9Se  →  2In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>  +  3SeO<sub>2</sub> since the two-step reaction process between In<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Se which produces In<sup>0</sup> transient phase may not occur after monitoring the products at different selenization temperature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The selenization procedure meanwhile accompanied the reduction of GO, leading to the formation of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6229-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a2c603f4-257f-488f-a0c5-cc2a599455f6/advs6229-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a2c603f4-257f-488f-a0c5-cc2a599455f6/advs6229-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e9b46586-2fd7-472a-b496-509f242e09bf/advs6229-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a2c603f4-257f-488f-a0c5-cc2a599455f6/advs6229-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6229-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202303108" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration for the preparation process of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6229-fig-0002">2</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) present the morphology of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites with different loading amount of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>. The selenization process resulted in an obvious size increase of the as-obtained In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs. Even for the sample with the lowest In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> loading amount (γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.2 nanocomposite), the particle size prominently increased from &lt;50&nbsp;nm for the In(OH)<sub>3</sub> precursor to several hundreds of nanometers for these In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs, which could be a result from the sintering effect and high reactivity of the adjacent In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs. A shape transformation of the cube-like particles into hexangular sheet-stacked morphology during the selenization process was also observed, which can be explained by the hexagonal crystalline nature of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3.</sub> In the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.2 nanocomposite, according to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a> (Supporting Information), the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs were homogeneously anchored on the wrinkled surface of rGO. Some tiny In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs were also detected. The high monodispersibility of rGO was a result from the cold-quenching and the following freeze-drying treatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0041_R_d149124960e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Alloys Compd.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increase of In(OH)<sub>3</sub> feedstock, the loading density of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> on the rGO matrix increased. For the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0002">2a,b</a>), the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs still maintained a uniform dispersion without obvious size increase, while the further augmented In(OH)<sub>3</sub> addition proportion (the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.01 nanocomposite) gave rise to poor dispersibility and serious sintering of the resulting In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a> (Supporting Information). Furthermore, the bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> sample derived from the direct selenization of In(OH)<sub>3</sub> NPs demonstrated a similar sintering behavior to that in the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.01 nanocomposite and an agglomerated morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4e,f</a>, Supporting Information ), indicating rGO played important roles in the dispersibility of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs. In addition, the electric conductivity of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> was prominently improved after the combination with rGO. As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the electric conductivity steadily rises from 0.010 S cm<sup>−1</sup> of bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> to 0.071 S cm<sup>−1</sup> of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.2 nanocomposite as increasing rGO content, further reflecting the favorable association state between In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs and rGO.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6229-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/22f6abd1-2794-4eb4-8d94-50207f6b2d77/advs6229-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/22f6abd1-2794-4eb4-8d94-50207f6b2d77/advs6229-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc9b2140-0935-40d9-b07d-425e1a84681f/advs6229-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/22f6abd1-2794-4eb4-8d94-50207f6b2d77/advs6229-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6229-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202303108" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM images, c) TEM, and d) HRTEM images of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite. e) The HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding EDX elemental mapping.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The weight proportions of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> in the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites were determined by TG analyses. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the weight loss below 200&nbsp;°C was related to the adsorbed or crystal water, while the slight mass increase before 400&nbsp;°C arose from the oxidation of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> to form In<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and SeO<sub>2</sub>. The subsequent stepwise weight loss was ascribed to the consumption of carbon and the sublimation of SeO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0042_R_d149124960e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J.                            <mi>percentage</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        </mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>In</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\frac{{{\mathrm{molar\ mass\ }}( {{\mathrm{In}}_2{\mathrm{Se}}_3} )}}{{{\mathrm{molar\ mass\ }}( {{\mathrm{In}}_2{{\mathrm{O}}}_3} )}} \times {\mathrm{weight\ percentage\ }}( {{\mathrm{In}}_2{{\mathrm{O}}}_3} )$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>. As a result, the percentages of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> in the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.2, γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 and γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.01 nanocomposites were estimated to be 54.5&nbsp;wt.%, 84.0&nbsp;wt.%, and 96.6&nbsp;wt.%, respectively. Correspondingly, the approximate proportions of rGO and PVP-derived pyrolysis carbon can be calculated, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Considering both high loading amount and favorable dispersibility of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> in the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite, its microstructure was further investigated by TEM and HRTEM. According to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0002">2c</a>, the wrinkle-rich surface characteristic and the thin lateral sheets of rGO were distinctly observed, which were resulted from the inclination to minimize surface energy during the cold-quenching and solvent-removal process. The wrinkled surface inhibited the restack and agglomeration of GO sheets. As a result, the monodispersed rGO was well retained after the heat treatment. It is also noted that the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs were firmly anchored on or wrapped in rGO, which was benefited from the cold-quenching by liquid N<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0043_R_d149124960e1257" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Eng. J.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The compact association between the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs and rGO was more clearly reflected in the HRTEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0002">2d</a>). In addition, the tiny crystals that expose different crystalline planes on surface were observed, implying the polycrystalline nature of the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs. The determined lattice fringe distances of 0.15, 0.29, and 0.31&nbsp;nm were indexed to (226), (202), and (113) planes for In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS 40–1407). The high angle annular dark field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) image and the corresponding EDX elemental mapping were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0002">2e</a>. The elemental dispersion of In and Se overlapped well, which concentrated in the white particle regions in the STEM image, intuitively proving the existence of pure In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> particles on the surface of rGO. The detected O element was attributed to residual oxygen functional groups on the surface of rGO after heat treatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0044_R_d149124960e1286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Carbon">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The crystalline phase compositions of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites were analyzed by XRD. It is seen from <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6229-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) that the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites and bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> had roughly the same patterns, indicating the introduction of the rGO matrix did not affect the transformation of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> phase from In(OH)<sub>3</sub>. The slightly lower peak intensity and increased peak width would be attributed to the reduced particle size of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and improved dispersity after the introduction of rGO. The diffraction peaks can be well indexed to mixed phases of γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, which consisted of a major phase (JCPDS 40–1407, <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 7.13 Å, <i>c</i> = 19.38 Å), and a minor phase (JCPDS 20–0494, <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 7.10 Å, <i>c</i> = 19.31 Å). The γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> had a hexagonal crystal structure with distorted wurtzite-type atomic arrangement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0045_R_d149124960e1348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - 2D Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The helical tetrahedral bonding characteristic in crystal cells is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). Raman spectra were further conducted to reveal the texture of the carbon components (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0003">3b</a>). For the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite, two distinct peaks at 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup> (G band) and 1360 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D band) were related to the lattice vibration from sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized ordered carbon atoms and defect or disordered structures in carbon materials, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0046_R_d149124960e1370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Carbon">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high intensity ratio of 0.86 between D band and G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) signifies the low restack degree of graphene layers and high surface or edge defects for facilitating the association with In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs. The two characteristic bands were also detected in the spectrum of bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, which were originated from the PVP-derived pyrolysis carbon. In addition, a discernible peak appearing at ≈143 cm<sup>−1</sup> was attributed to the A<sub>1g</sub><sup>1</sup> phonon vibration mode of γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0047_R_d149124960e1401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mol. Struct.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6229-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1d4ba957-3397-43da-a444-c9ceb707f926/advs6229-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1d4ba957-3397-43da-a444-c9ceb707f926/advs6229-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/44061b5c-3ba5-4418-b1c2-e1d2ef60a33c/advs6229-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1d4ba957-3397-43da-a444-c9ceb707f926/advs6229-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6229-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202303108" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite and bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>. The high-resolution XPS spectra of In 3d c), Se 3d d), and C 1s e) for the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite. f) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite and bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The surface chemical composition and bonding states of elements were measured by XPS. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the XPS survey spectrum of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite manifested the distinct characteristic signals of C, In, Se, and O elements. The elemental compositions were consistent with the results of XRD and Raman spectrum analyses discussed above. In the deconvoluted In 3d spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0003">3c</a>), the peaks located at 444.6&nbsp;eV and 452.0&nbsp;eV were assigned to In 3d<sub>2/5</sub> and In 3d<sub>2/3</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0048_R_d149124960e1466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fine-scanned Se 3d spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0003">3d</a>) can be deconvoluted into two peaks at 54.1&nbsp;eV and 55.6&nbsp;eV, which were corresponded to Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Se<sup>2−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0049_R_d149124960e1481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the high-resolution C 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0003">3e</a>), the peaks appearing at 285.2&nbsp;eV, 286.4&nbsp;eV, 288.6&nbsp;eV, and 289.1&nbsp;eV signified the existence forms of C = C/C─C, C─O, C = O, and O─C = O in the carbon components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0050_R_d149124960e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Carbon">50</a>, <a href="#advs6229-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0051_R_d149124960e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nanoscale">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The pore structure characteristics of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite and bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> were determined by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms. The bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> presented a type III-IV mixed isotherm with a small adsorption-desorption hysteresis loop at high relative pressure, indicating the coexistence of macropores and mesopores, due to the stacking of the irregular In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> particles which were interconnected by sintering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0052_R_d149124960e1515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the incorporation of the rGO matrix, a prominent pore structure change was observed. As is seen from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6229-fig-0003">3f</a> that the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite possessed a typical IV type isotherm. The emerged distinct H<sub>3</sub> hysteresis loop in the high-to-medium pressure range (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>) of 0.45–0.95 was indicative of the existence of rich mesopores in materials. The corresponding pore size distribution curves further proved that the average size of mesopores in the bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> shifts from ≈41.0 to ≈4.3&nbsp;nm accompanying a higher pore distribution concentration after the proper addition of rGO. In addition, the Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) specific surface area of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 nanocomposite and bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> were determined to be 17.5 and 8.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These results all reflect the positive effect of the introduction of the rGO matrix, which can provide much improved dispersibility for In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> NPs and properly enlarged specific surface for increased surface reaction sites and elevated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6229-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6229-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Behaviors of the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO Nanocomposites</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical behaviors of the various γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO nanocomposites and the bare In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> were investigated in CR2025 coin-type half cells with a sodium metal foil as the counter electrode. All the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO electrodes displayed similar CV profiles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6229-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, Supporting Information) in the initial cathodic/anodic scans, signifying the analogous redox reaction processes. For the γ-In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@rGO-0.06 electrode, two irreversible cathodic peaks, that emerged at 0.90&nbsp;V and 0.69&nbsp;V in the first scan, were attributed to electrolyte decomposition and the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0053_R_d149124960e1599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem. Eng. J.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the second cycle on, three reversible peaks at 0.95&nbsp;V, 0.64&nbsp;V, and 0.32&nbsp;V were clearly observed, which were the indications of the consecutive intercalation, conversion, and alloying reactions during the interaction between In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>, respectively. Correspondingly, the sharp oxidation peak at 0.42&nbsp;V and the broad one at 1.44&nbsp;V in the anodic scans were ascribed to the stepwise desodiation reactions. The related reaction process is summarized below:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6229-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6229-bib-0036_R_d149124960e1611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="advs6229-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9ae541a4-90d9-4aca-8363-3be1e8ed5cd4/advs6229-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6229:advs6229-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/advs6229-math-0005.png">
